(Paper)
<span class="italic"><a title="Link to journal home page" href="https://doi.org/10.1039/2050-7496/2013">J. Mater. Chem. A</a></span>, 2024, <strong>12</strong>, 30629-30641</div><h1 id="sect188"><span class="title_heading">Sodium storage performance of a high entropy sulfide anode with reduced volume expansion<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a></span></h1><p class="header_text">
      <span class="bold">
        
          
            Jianping 
            Ma<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn2">‡</a>
          
        
        
      </span>, 
      <span class="bold">
        
          
            Jinyi 
            Guo<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn2">‡</a>
          
        
      </span>, 
      <span class="bold">
        
          
            Weizheng 
            Li
          
        
      </span>, 
      <span class="bold">
        
          
            Xiaohan 
            Yang
          
        
      </span><span class="bold"> and </span>
      <span class="bold">
        
          
            Chengde 
            Huang
          
        
      </span><span class="orcid"><a target="_blank" title="Select to open ORCID record for Chengde&nbsp;Huang (orcid.org/0000-0003-3719-4470) in a new window" id="connect-orcid-link" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0003-3719-4470"><img id="orcid-id-logo" src="/content/NewImages/orcid_16x16.png" alt="ORCID logo"></a></span>*
      <br><span class="italic">Department of Applied Chemistry, School of Chemical Engineering and Technology, Tianjin University, Tianjin, 300072, P.R. China. E-mail: <a href="mailto:cdhuang@tju.edu.cn">cdhuang@tju.edu.cn</a></span>
    </p><div id="art-admin"><span class="italic bold">Received 
      23rd July 2024
    </span><span class="bold italic">, Accepted 12th October 2024</span></div><p class="bold italic">First published on 14th October 2024</p><hr><div class="abstract"><h2>Abstract</h2><p>Metal sulfides are prominent candidates for sodium-ion battery (SIB) anodes owing to their high theoretical capacities and superior conductivities, but their performance is hindered by volume expansion during cycling. This study introduces an approach for mitigating these issues by incorporating high-entropy structures into sulfides. We synthesized a high-entropy sulfide (HES) (FeCoNiCuZn)In<small><sub>2</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small> (MS5) and medium- and low-entropy sulfides for comparison. Physical and chemical characterization confirmed the successful formation of the HES, the uniform distribution of elements and the presence of sulfur vacancies. We show that high-entropy doping alleviates volume expansion during cycling and enhances sodium storage capacity, thereby improving electrochemical performance. After 800 cycles at a current density of 1 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, MS5 exhibits a reversible capacity of 412.7 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. When the current density is increased to 5 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, it can still stably cycle for 800 cycles with a capacity retention rate of up to 88%. Density functional theory calculations supported the experimental findings, indicating that the introduction of high-entropy structures enhances the structural stability and Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> migration, and increases the number of reactive sites. This study highlights the potential of HES materials for use in the anodes of next-generation SIBs, offering insights into their design and application in improved energy-storage solutions.</p></div><hr>
    
      
      <h2 id="sect244"><span class="a_heading">1 Introduction</span></h2>
      <span>The development and utilisation of non-renewable resources for economic growth results in various problems, such as resource depletion, environmental pollution, and global warming.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit1"><sup><span class="sup_ref">1–3</span></sup></a> It is estimated that at the current rate of extraction, global reserves of coal, oil, and natural gas will last for less than 130 years.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit4"><sup><span class="sup_ref">4</span></sup></a> Therefore, the development of renewable energy sources has been a research focus for decades.</span>
      <p class="otherpara">However, due to the disadvantages of renewables, such as uneven location distribution and unstable power, there is a growing demand for more efficient and longer-life energy storage systems. Although lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have advantages such as a long cycle life and no memory effect, the rapid development of lithium mining is depleting lithium resources, greatly increasing the production cost of LIBs. More importantly, LIBs exhibit poor safety performance, with incidents of battery self-ignition and explosions frequently occurring in the automotive industry.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit5"><sup><span class="sup_ref">5</span></sup></a> Due to their low cost from using abundant raw materials and inherent safety, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have increasingly attracted the interest of researchers.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit6"><sup><span class="sup_ref">6,7</span></sup></a> However, because of the large ionic radius of Na, anode materials suitable for LIBs cannot be used in SIBs. In recent years, researchers have attempted to improve the cycle life and capacity of SIBs by exploring various anode materials.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit8"><sup><span class="sup_ref">8,9</span></sup></a></p>
      <p class="otherpara">Metal sulfides have high theoretical specific capacities, abundant redox centres, and high electron conductivities, making them candidate materials for SIB anodes. However, metal-sulfide anode materials experience significant volume expansion during cycling, leading to a low rate capability and shortened cycle life.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit10"><sup><span class="sup_ref">10</span></sup></a> This is mainly because the deintercalation of sodium ions causes the collapse of the crystal structure and leads to continual fracturing, regeneration, and refracturing of the solid-electrolyte interphase (SEI) film, resulting in irreversible capacity loss. In order to tackle the aforementioned problem and improve the performance of metal sulfides, researchers have attempted to reduce the material particle size, and apply carbon coatings and atomic doping.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit11"><sup><span class="sup_ref">11,12</span></sup></a> Krengel <span class="italic">et al.</span><a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit13"><sup><span class="sup_ref">13</span></sup></a> developed CuV<small><sub>2</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small> as an anode for SIBs, which exhibited a high reversible capacity of 580 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> after 300 cycles at 0.7 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. Hsia <span class="italic">et al.</span><a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit14"><sup><span class="sup_ref">14</span></sup></a> developed a hexagonal ZnIn<small><sub>2</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>/C anode for SIBs that exhibited a high capacity of 560 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 3.2 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>; however, its capacity retention rate dropped to 62% after 500 cycles. Despite achieving encouraging results, further cycling resulted in nanoparticle aggregation and structural degradation, leading to irreversible conversion reactions and poor cycle performance.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit15"><sup><span class="sup_ref">15</span></sup></a></p>
      <p class="otherpara">High-entropy sulfides (HESs) are a new class of materials, defined as near-equimolar sulfides composed of five or more elements.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit16"><sup><span class="sup_ref">16–18</span></sup></a> They have been developed and studied in recent years and are now widely used in the field of electrochemistry.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit19"><sup><span class="sup_ref">19</span></sup></a> High-entropy structures can help maintain stable crystal structures and increase the reactivity of the active sites during electrochemical energy storage. Designing high-entropy structures in sulfides to alleviate volume changes during cycling is an excellent strategy. Lin <span class="italic">et al.</span><a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit20"><sup><span class="sup_ref">20</span></sup></a> synthesized a HES (FeMnNiCoCr)S<small><sub>2</sub></small> and used it in LIBs for the first time. After 200 cycles at 1 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, the specific capacity of (FeMnNiCoCr)S<small><sub>2</sub></small> was still 600 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, with a coulombic efficiency (CE) of 98%. Theibault <span class="italic">et al.</span><a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit21"><sup><span class="sup_ref">21</span></sup></a> developed Zn<small><sub>0.30</sub></small>Co<small><sub>0.31</sub></small>Cu<small><sub>0.19</sub></small>In<small><sub>0.13</sub></small>Ga<small><sub>0.06</sub></small>S coated on glassy carbon as an anode for lithium–sulfur (Li–S) batteries. The initial capacity of the battery at a discharge rate of 0.2C was 706 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> and after 100 cycles the battery capacity was 480 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> (retention rate of 68%).</p>
      <p class="otherpara">HESs are rarely used as anode materials in SIBs. Fe, Co, and Ni are typical transition metals, and these elements exhibit multiple oxidation states, such as Fe<small><sup>2+</sup></small>/Fe<small><sup>3+</sup></small>, Co<small><sup>2+</sup></small>/Co<small><sup>3+</sup></small>, and Ni<small><sup>2+</sup></small>/Ni<small><sup>3+</sup></small>. Through the conversion between these valence states, they can facilitate multi-electron transfer during charge and discharge processes. Multi-electron transfer means that the material can store or release more charge per unit mass, significantly enhancing the battery's capacity and energy density. The introduction of Cu and Zn can form stable metal sulfide phases, which effectively disperse stress and help mitigate the volume changes in the material, thereby improving structural stability and cycle life. Incorporating various elements can lead to changes in the bandgap and create synergistic effects, optimizing the electrochemical reaction pathways. This results in higher conductivity and faster reaction kinetics in practical applications. In this study, we introduced elements such as Co, Ni, Cu, and Zn into the tetrahedral sites of FeIn<small><sub>2</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>, successfully synthesising the HES (FeCoNiCuZn)In<small><sub>2</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small> (MS5). Furthermore, other sulfide materials, including FeIn<small><sub>2</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small> (MS1), (FeCo)In<small><sub>2</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small> (MS2), (FeCoNi)In<small><sub>2</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small> (MS3), and (FeCoNiCu)In<small><sub>2</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small> (MS4), were prepared for comparison with the HES. The theoretical specific capacities of MS1–MS5 are 518.2, 516.3, 515.8, 514 and 512.5 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, respectively. The sodium storage behaviours of the five sulfide materials were then analysed. Subsequently, the electronic density of states (DOS), band structure, and sodium intercalation mechanism of HESs were analyzed through density functional theory (DFT) calculations. The findings revealed that the formation of high-entropy structures alleviates volume expansion during cycling and increases the sodium storage capacity.</p>
    
    
      
      <h2 id="sect308"><span class="a_heading">2 Experimental section</span></h2>
      
        
        <h3 id="sect311"><span class="b_heading">2.1 Synthesis of materials</span></h3>
        <div>
          
          <span id="sect314"><span class="c_heading_indent">2.1.1 Preparation of (FeCoNiCuZn)In<small><sub>2</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>. </span>
          <span>HES was successfully synthesized using a simple co-precipitation followed by a high-temperature sintering method. First, 0.03038 g of ferrous sulfate (FeSO<small><sub>4</sub></small>), 0.02597 g of cobalt chloride (CoCl<small><sub>2</sub></small>), 0.02592 g of nickel chloride (NiCl<small><sub>2</sub></small>), 0.0499 g of copper sulfate pentahydrate (CuSO<small><sub>4</sub></small>·5H<small><sub>2</sub></small>O), 0.02726 g of zinc chloride (ZnCl<small><sub>2</sub></small>), 0.442 g of indium chloride (InCl<small><sub>3</sub></small>), and 0.2941 g of sodium citrate (C<small><sub>6</sub></small>H<small><sub>5</sub></small>Na<small><sub>3</sub></small>O<small><sub>7</sub></small>) were added to 40 ml of deionized water. Next, 1.667 ml of concentrated HCl was added to create a strong acidic atmosphere. After stirring for 30 minutes, 1 M sodium sulfide solution (Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>S) was slowly added dropwise at 80 °C to adjust the pH to 6. The resulting precipitate was centrifuged, washed, and then dried in a vacuum oven at 80 °C for 12 hours. Finally, the dried blocks were milled and then subjected to annealing at 500 °C for 2 hours in an argon (Ar) atmosphere using a tube furnace, resulting in the synthesis of the MS5 material. The synthesis of MS1–MS4 sulfide materials followed the preparation process of MS5, with only differences in the proportions and types of added elements (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1</a>).</span>
          <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig1"><a href="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta05122j/d4ta05122j-f1_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta05122j-f1.tif" src="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta05122j/d4ta05122j-f1.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 1 </b> <span id="fig1"><span class="graphic_title">Schematic diagram of synthesis of MS1–MS5 electrode materials.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        </span></div>
      
      
        
        <h3 id="sect335"><span class="b_heading">2.2 Characterization</span></h3>
        <span>This article uses the D8 Focus X-ray diffractometer (XRD) model from the German company Bruker, with a copper target as the radiation source (<span class="italic">λ</span> = 0.154 nm), a scanning range of 20–80°, and a scanning speed of 2° min<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. The obtained XRD patterns were refined using the GSAS II software. The microstructure of metal sulfides was analyzed using an S-4800 SEM from the Japanese company Hitachi, transmission electron microscopy (TEM) images were captured with a Talos F200X and the elemental distribution on the surface of metal sulfides was detected through energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS). The interactions between atoms in HESs were analyzed using a laser confocal Raman spectrometer produced by the French company Horiba, with a laser wavelength of 532 nm. Inductively coupled plasma optical emission spectroscopy (ICP-OES) was employed for element quantification, utilizing an Agilent 5800 ICP-OES. A Thermo Fisher Scientific K-Alpha+ X-ray photoelectron spectrometer (XPS) was employed to investigate the valence states and compositions of elements in HESs before and after cycling. The instrument uses Al Kα as the X-ray source, with carbon C 1s (284.80 eV as the calibration value) for binding energy calibration, and operates under a vacuum condition of 5 × 10<small><sup>−8</sup></small> Pa.</span>
      
      
        
        <h3 id="sect342"><span class="b_heading">2.3 Electrochemical measurements</span></h3>
        <div>
          
          <span id="sect345"><span class="c_heading_indent">2.3.1 Half-cell assembly. </span>
          <span>2032 button batteries were used for electrochemical performance testing. 14 mm diameter sodium foil was used as the working electrode, 1 M NaPF<small><sub>6</sub></small> (diethylene glycol dimethyl ether (DEGDME)) was used as the electrolyte, and glass fiber (Whatman GF/D) was used as the separator; button batteries were assembled in a glovebox with an argon atmosphere, and charge–discharge tests were conducted over a voltage range of 0.01–2.5 V.</span>
        </span></div>
        <div>
          
          <span id="sect350"><span class="c_heading_indent">2.3.2 The whole battery assembly. </span>
          <span>The positive electrode of the whole battery is obtained by thoroughly grinding a mixture of Na<small><sub>3</sub></small>V<small><sub>2</sub></small>(PO<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>3</sub></small> (NVP), Super P, and polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) solution (5% PVDF solution formed by dissolving PVDF in 1-methyl-2-pyrrolidinone (NMP) solvent) in a ratio of 7<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">2<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">:<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">1, and coating it on Al foil. To ensure the performance of the whole battery, the anode material must first be activated in a half-cell. Then, using 1 M NaClO<small><sub>4</sub></small> (PC + 5% FEC) as the electrolyte, the whole battery is assembled by pairing the anode with NVP.</span>
        </span></div>
        <div>
          
          <span id="sect359"><span class="c_heading_indent">2.3.3 Electrochemical performance characterization methods. </span>
          <span>An electrochemical workstation (CHI660E) was used to perform cyclic voltammetry (CV), electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS), and the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT). The cycling performance and rate performance of the battery were tested using the LANHE CT3002A by conducting constant current discharge–charge under different current densities. The distribution of relaxation times (DRT) tools visual software developed by Zhang <span class="italic">et al.</span><a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit22"><sup><span class="sup_ref">22</span></sup></a> is used for DRT analysis of EIS. The relationship between the scan rate and peak current in CV curves can provide information on the rate-controlling steps in electrochemical reactions. The CV curves show the relationship between the peak current and scan rate:<table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn1"><span id="eqn1"><span class="italic">i</span> = <span class="italic">aν</span><small><sup><span class="italic">b</span></sup></small></span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(1)</td></tr></tbody></table><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn2"><span id="eqn2">log(<span class="italic">i</span>) = <span class="italic">b</span><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">log(<span class="italic">v</span>) + log(<span class="italic">a</span>)</span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(2)</td></tr></tbody></table>where <span class="italic">i</span> is the peak current, <span class="italic">v</span> is the scan rate, and <span class="italic">a</span> and <span class="italic">b</span> are constants. The speed control steps in electrochemical reactions can usually be determined based on the value of <span class="italic">b</span>. When <span class="italic">b</span> = 0.5, the reaction rate is controlled by the diffusion step, and when <span class="italic">b</span> = 1, the reaction is controlled by surface capacitance.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit23"><sup><span class="sup_ref">23</span></sup></a></span>
        </span></div>
      
      
        
        <h3 id="sect387"><span class="b_heading">2.4 DFT calculation</span></h3>
        <span>The Castep module in Materials Studio was used to establish the FeIn<small><sub>2</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small> cubic crystal system as the reference model, and configurations of five materials (FeCo)In<small><sub>2</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>, (FeCoNi)In<small><sub>2</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>, (FeCoNiCu)In<small><sub>2</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>, (FeCoNiCuZn)In<small><sub>2</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>, and (FeCoNiZnMn)In<small><sub>2</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small> were constructed. The configurations were geometrically optimized using cutoff energy and <span class="italic">k</span>-point convergence tests, with final converged data. Geometric optimization utilized the GGA-PBE functional, OTFG ultrasoft pseudopotentials for calculations, and band structure, DOS, and differential charge density analyses were performed on the optimized configurations. Additionally, simulations of the reaction potential and corresponding volume changes during intercalation reactions in HES discharge processes were conducted.</span>
        <p class="otherpara">The DFT calculation of the formation energy of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> with different quantities inserted is as follows:</p><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn3"><span id="eqn3">Δ<span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>f</sub></small> = <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>(Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>MS5)</sub></small> − <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>(MS5)</sub></small> − <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>(Na)</sub></small></span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(3)</td></tr></tbody></table><p></p>
        <p class="otherpara">According to the formula for calculating the reaction potential of HESs:</p><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn4"><a href="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta05122j/d4ta05122j-t1_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta05122j-t1.tif" src="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta05122j/d4ta05122j-t1.gif"></a></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(4)</td></tr></tbody></table><span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>(Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>MS5)</sub></small> is the total energy of HESs combined with Na, <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>(MS5)</sub></small> is the energy of HES, and <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>(Na)</sub></small> is the energy of Na after normalization.<p></p>
      
    
    
      
      <h2 id="sect431"><span class="a_heading">3 Results and discussion</span></h2>
      
        
        <h3 id="sect434"><span class="b_heading">3.1 Characterization of HESs</span></h3>
        <span>
          <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2a</a> shows the refined XRD patterns of MS5 in the 20–80° range, respectively. The XRD spectra of the medium- and low-entropy sulfides are shown in Fig. S1.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> Characteristic peaks at 23.68°, 27.84°, 33.73°, 44.29°, 48.45°, and 56.81° correspond to the (220), (311), (400), (511), (440), and (533) crystal planes of FeIn<small><sub>2</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>, respectively.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit24"><sup><span class="sup_ref">24</span></sup></a> Additionally, the XRD patterns of all samples showed no peak splitting or impurity peaks, indicating that doping did not alter the crystal structure of the material. Therefore, for further refinement, the crystal structure of the <span class="italic">Fd</span><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_0033_0304.gif" alt="[3 with combining macron]"><span class="italic">m</span> cubic space group (JCPDS-80-0608) was selected as a reference model. The refined XRD patterns of MS1–MS5 show that the errors are within an acceptable range<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit25"><sup><span class="sup_ref">25</span></sup></a> (<span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>w</sub></small> ≤ 6%, GOF ≤ 2), highlighting the reliability of the refinement results. The parameters and model obtained from Rietveld refinement are presented in <a title="Select to navigate to table" href="#tab1">Table 1</a> and Fig. S2.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> The results indicate that MS1 has an In–S bond length of 2.683 Å, a Fe–S bond length of 2.180 Å, unit-cell parameters of <span class="italic">a</span> = <span class="italic">b</span> = <span class="italic">c</span> = 10.508 Å, and a unit cell volume of 1160.32 Å<small><sup>3</sup></small>. Fe, In, and S occupy the 8b, 16c, and 32e sites of the crystal, respectively. For MS5, the In–S bond length is 2.648 Å, the average X–S (X = Fe, Co, <span class="italic">etc.</span>) bond length is 2.260 Å, the unit cell parameters are <span class="italic">a</span> = <span class="italic">b</span> = <span class="italic">c</span> = 10.540 Å, and the unit cell volume is 1170.94 Å<small><sup>3</sup></small>. X, In, and S occupy the 8b, 16c, and 32e sites of the crystal, respectively (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2b</a>). The substitution of Fe with Co, Ni, Cu, and Zn atoms resulted in a disordered atomic arrangement at the 8b site and variations in the bond energy when bonded with S, resulting in lattice distortion in the MS5 material. Moreover, the introduction of a high-entropy structure increases the unit cell volume, providing wider ion-transport channels, which is expected to promote the transport of electrons and ions.</span>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig2"><a href="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta05122j/d4ta05122j-f2_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta05122j-f2.tif" src="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta05122j/d4ta05122j-f2.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 2 </b> <span id="fig2"><span class="graphic_title">(a) Rietveld refinement of MS5. (b) Crystal schematic of the MS5 material. (c) Raman spectra of MS1–MS5 materials. (d) TEM image of MS5. (e) TEM-EDS of MS5.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>

            </table></div></div>
          <hr class="hrule"><br>
        <p class="otherpara">
          <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2c</a> shows the Raman spectra of MS1–MS5. The characteristic peaks at around 249, 315, and 364 cm<small><sup>−1</sup></small> correspond to the A<small><sub>1g</sub></small>, E<small><sub>g</sub></small>, and B<small><sub>2g</sub></small> vibrational modes of the sulfide materials, respectively. The A<small><sub>1g</sub></small> peak is primarily due to the M–S (M = Fe, Co, Ni, Cu, Zn, In) stretching vibrations within the crystal, the E<small><sub>g</sub></small> peak is due to the In–S stretching vibrations, and the B<small><sub>2g</sub></small> peak is attributed to the vibrations of the S atoms within the crystal.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit26"><sup><span class="sup_ref">26,27</span></sup></a> By increasing the number of dopant elements, the E<small><sub>g</sub></small> peak exhibits a blue shift, the B<small><sub>2g</sub></small> peak shows a blue shift followed by a red shift, and the A1g peak shows a red-shift. Additionally, distinct A<small><sub>1g</sub></small>, E<small><sub>g</sub></small>, and B<small><sub>2g</sub></small> peaks were observed for MS1, MS2, and MS3, whereas only the E<small><sub>g</sub></small> peak was prominent for MS4 and MS5. This indicates that the introduction of more than four metal elements leads to a rapid increase in the local disorder of M–S bonds within the crystal, likely due to lattice distortion from the random arrangement of atoms. Correspondingly, the intensity of the E<small><sub>g</sub></small> peak increased, indicating that the atomic arrangement around the In–S bonds became more ordered, thereby maintaining the stability of the crystal structure.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">
          <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2d</a> shows the TEM image of the MS5 powder sample, from which clear lattice stripes can be observed. The adjacent lattice stripes with 0.325 nm and 0.183 nm spacing correspond to the (311) and (440) crystal planes of MS5, which is fully consistent with the XRD results (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2a</a>), indicating the successful synthesis of the HES sample. The MS5 HES primarily consists of nanosheets ranging from tens to hundreds of nanometres in size, with all elements in MS5 uniformly distributed on the surface, without any elemental aggregation or segregation (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2e</a>). The morphologies and distributions of the medium- and low-entropy sulfides are shown in Fig. S3.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> Maximizing the structural entropy of the HES requires ensuring that the transition metals are distributed in equimolar or almost equimolar ratios. We conducted atomic ratio analysis of the transition metal elements in MS2–MS5 using ICP-OES. The results showed that the elements forming the high-entropy structure (Fe, Co, Ni, Cu, and Zn) were present in nearly equimolar amounts (Table S1<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>). However, due to the requirement for ICP-OES samples to be in a weakly acidic or neutral solution, a small portion of Fe<small><sup>3+</sup></small> in the sample formed Fe(OH)<small><sub>3</sub></small> precipitate during sample preparation and was filtered out. As a result, the measured Fe content was slightly lower than that of the other elements. The introduction of a high-entropy structure reduces the crystal size of the material, which helps shorten the diffusion path of sodium ions during the charging and discharging processes, accelerates the sodium-ion transport rate, and improves the sodium storage performance of the HES.</p>
      
      
        
        <h3 id="sect531"><span class="b_heading">3.2 Sodium storage performance</span></h3>
        <span>Using metallic Na as the counter electrode and 1 M NaPF<small><sub>6</sub></small> (DEGDME) as the electrolyte, 2032-type cells were assembled. The CV curves of MS1–MS5 obtained at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<small><sup>−1</sup></small> indicate similar electrochemical behaviours among the five sulfides (Fig. S4<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>). As shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3a</a>, during the first discharge process, a distinct reduction peak appears at around 0.6 V in the CV curve of MS5, which is attributed to the insertion of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> into MS5 to form the intermediate phase Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>MS5 and the formation of Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>S, metal nanoparticles and a SEI layer through a Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>MS5 conversion reaction. This could also be accompanied by an alloying reaction between Zn and Na.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit14"><sup><span class="sup_ref">14,28,29</span></sup></a> The redox peaks at around 0 V are contributed by Super P.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit30"><sup><span class="sup_ref">30</span></sup></a> During the charging process, the oxidation peaks at around 1.15 and 2 V are attributed to the de-alloying and conversion of Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>S back to the Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>MS5 phase and the extraction of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>. A significant loss in irreversible capacity can be attributed to the formation of the SEI layer, which causes a notable difference between the initial and subsequent CV curves at the reduction peaks.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit31"><sup><span class="sup_ref">31</span></sup></a> However, the CV curves of the second and third cycles show good reproducibility, indicating that the material exhibits good reversibility.</span>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig3"><a href="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta05122j/d4ta05122j-f3_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta05122j-f3.tif" src="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta05122j/d4ta05122j-f3.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 3 </b> <span id="fig3"><span class="graphic_title">(a) The first three CV curves of MS5 materials at 0.1 mV s<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. (b) Charge–discharge curves of MS5 materials at 0.5 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. (c) Long-term cycling performance of MS1–MS5 at 1 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. (d) Rate capabilities of five sulfide materials at different current densities. (e) Long-term cycle stability of the MS5 material at a high current density of 5 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> (illustration: TEM image of MS5 after long-term cycling).</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <p class="otherpara">To further understand the cycling stability of the HES, several sulfides were subjected to constant-current charge–discharge testing. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3b</a> shows the charge–discharge curves of MS5 for the 1st, 2nd, 10th, 50th, and 100th cycles at a current density of 0.5 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. The voltage platforms of the charge–discharge curves for the five sulfides correspond well to the redox peaks of the CV curves (the charge–discharge curves of medium- and low-entropy sulfides are shown in Fig. S5<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>). MS5 exhibits the highest initial charge–discharge specific capacities, reaching 616.3 and 526.9 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, respectively, with an initial CE (ICE) of 85.59%. In comparison, MS1 had an initial charge/discharge specific capacity of 554.4/477.4 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> with an ICE of 86.83%, MS2 had 607.8/509.9 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> with an ICE of 83.58%, MS3 had 597.2/513.4 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> with an ICE of 87.26%, and MS4 had 587.3/506.7 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> with an ICE of 86.41%. After 100 cycles, the capacity retention rate remained at 96.5%, indicating the excellent cycling stability of MS5. The high initial CE is likely due to the good compatibility between the sulfide and ether-based electrolyte, which forms a thin and dense SEI layer, thereby reducing irreversible capacity loss.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit32"><sup><span class="sup_ref">32</span></sup></a> Subsequently, we conducted XRD tests (Fig. S6<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>) to investigate whether MS5 would separate into the corresponding metal sulfides of each metal due to structural instability during sodiation/desodiation. The results show that at a voltage of 2.5 V, the sodium embedding process has not yet occurred in the anode of the half-cell, and the lattice morphology of the active material loaded on the Cu collector has not been changed, and its XRD diffraction peaks are relatively obvious. As the reaction proceeds, Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> enters the MS5 material in an irregular form, transforming it into an amorphous state and a (511) plane. Since Cu is a collector, the intensity of the characteristic peaks of the Cu element in the XRD spectrum is so high that the characteristic peaks of the (511) crystalline plane of MS5 are not clearly visible. In addition, no other single metal sulfides were found to be generated during the sodiation/desodiation process.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">The long-term cycling performances of the five sulfide materials were tested at a current density of 1 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. MS1 has the worst cycling performance (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3c</a>), with its capacity rapidly declining from 472.5 to 187.3 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> after 130 cycles. Based on the analysis of Fig. S7<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> and <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">3d</a>, MS1 exhibited relatively stable cycling performance over the first 100 cycles at a current density of 0.5 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. When the current density was increased to 1 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, the specific capacity of the battery showed an initial decrease followed by an increase. However, under the condition of 2 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, 5 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> and 10 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, the electrochemical performance of MS1 revealed a rapid decay in specific capacity within 100 cycles. Furthermore, the EDS map of MS1 after 100 cycles at 1 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> indicated a significant decrease in sulfur content (Fig. S8 and S9<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>). Therefore, we hypothesize that the rapid capacity decay observed in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3c</a> is due to the poor rate performance of MS1 and the loss of sulfur from FeIn<small><sub>2</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small> during cycling, leading to structural degradation of MS1. During subsequent cycles, MS1 exhibits an electrochemical activation phenomenon, possibly due to the diffusion of Cu from the current collector into the anode electrode, which then participates in the electrochemical reaction;<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit33"><sup><span class="sup_ref">33,34</span></sup></a> the results of a comparative experiment using Al foil as the current collector are shown in Fig. S10.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> As shown in Fig. S8, S9 and S11–S15,<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> EDS analysis of the MS1 and MS5 electrode sheets before and after cycling confirmed the occurrence of Cu deposition during cycling (significant Cu deposition was also observed on the electrode sheets of MS2–4 after cycling). Notably, the sulfur content in MS5 did not decrease significantly during cycling, which can be attributed to the stability and reversibility of the high-entropy structure. Additionally, when comparing the collapse of the active material on the surface of the electrodes after cycling (Fig. S22<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>), no exposed Cu current collector was observed in MS5, further demonstrating that the high-entropy structure is markedly superior to the medium- and low-entropy structures. In contrast, MS5 maintains a reversible capacity of 412.7 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> after 800 cycles at 1 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, with a capacity retention of 83.2% and a CE above 99%. This demonstrates that the high-entropy structure mitigates the volume changes in sulfides during cycling, ensuring the structural stability of the crystal, and thereby extending the lifespan of the battery.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">To investigate the sodium-ion transport rate in the HES anode, the rate capabilities of MS1–MS5 were measured at different current densities. MS5 maintains a significant reversible capacity of 323.4 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> even at a significant current density of 10 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3d</a>). When the current density is subsequently reduced to 0.5 and 0.2 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> and cycled 10 and 25 times, respectively, MS5 still exhibits reversible capacities of 485.4 and 471.5 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, respectively. In contrast, MS1 shows lower rate capabilities than the other sulfide materials at all current densities. This is likely because of the lower conductivity of MS1 compared to the other materials and its inability to withstand the volume changes caused by sodium-ion insertion and extraction during cycling. The rapid charge–discharge stability of the HES was further tested at a current density of 5 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. The results show that after 800 stable cycles, MS5 maintains a high specific capacity of 401.8 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3e</a>) and a capacity retention rate of 88%, with a CE greater than 99%. From the illustration in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3e</a>, it can be seen that after long-term cycling, MS5 still exhibits an aggregated state of irregular small crystals, ranging from tens to hundreds of nanometers. Since the TEM samples were taken from the cycled electrode sheets, they are surrounded by the conductive agent Super-P. In another illustration, MS5 primarily presents the (511) crystal plane, with a slight increase in the interplanar spacing. This indicates that MS5 undergoes crystal plane reorganization and experiences slight volume expansion after charge–discharge cycling, which is consistent with the XRD results obtained during cycling. Additionally, there are blurred areas in the image where lattice fringes cannot be resolved, attributed to the obstruction caused by the outer Super-P coating. Notably, the cycling stability and rate capability of MS5 at high current densities are significantly superior to those of most reported metal sulfide materials for SIBs<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit35"><sup><span class="sup_ref">35–37</span></sup></a> (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4d</a> and Table S2<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>).</p>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig4"><a href="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta05122j/d4ta05122j-f4_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta05122j-f4.tif" src="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta05122j/d4ta05122j-f4.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 4 </b> <span id="fig4"><span class="graphic_title">(a) CV curves of MS5 materials at 0.2, 0.5, 1, and 2 mV s<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. (b) Linear relationship between the scan rate and peak current of the MS5 material. (c) Percentage of the pseudocapacitance contribution of the MS5 material at different scan rates. (d) Comparison of the electrochemical performance of MS5 with recently reported metal sulfide materials for SIBs. Details are listed in Table S2.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> (e) Statistic results of <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>ct</sub></small> values in DRT analysis. (f) Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> diffusion coefficient of the MS1 and MS5 electrodes obtained from GITT curves. (g) Rate capability of the full cell. (h) Cycling performance of the full cell.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <p class="otherpara">The surface kinetic behaviour during sodium storage was investigated by measuring the CV curves of MS1 and MS5 at different scan rates (Fig. S16a<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> and <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">4a</a>, respectively). As the scan rate increases, the peak position of MS5 remains almost unchanged. This could be due to the rapid diffusion of sodium ions in MS5, which is further confirmed by the subsequent GITT test results. The corresponding <span class="italic">b</span> values were fitted using <a title="" href="#eqn1">eqn (1)</a>. For MS1 and MS5, all <span class="italic">b</span> values fall between 0.5 and 1 (Fig. S16b<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> and <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">4b</a>, respectively), indicating that the Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> insertion/extraction process is controlled by both surface capacitance and diffusion. Fig. S17<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> shows the pseudocapacitive contributions of MS1 and MS5. For MS1, the Na storage reactions at the reduction peak (0.4 V) and oxidation peak (1.2 V) are primarily controlled by the diffusion process. In contrast, for MS5, only the oxidation peak reaction is diffusion-controlled, whereas other processes are dominated by pseudocapacitance. As the scan rate increases, the pseudocapacitive contribution increases. As shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4c</a>, the pseudocapacitive contributions for MS5 at 0.2, 0.5, 1, and 2 mV s<small><sup>−1</sup></small> are 95.33%, 96.60%, 97.89%, and 98.57%, respectively, which are higher than those of MS1 at the same scan rates (Fig. S18<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>). This indicates that the vacancies, defects, dislocations, and other distortions generated by the high-entropy effect create more active sites on the surface of the material and enhance the reaction activity, thereby increasing sodium-ion storage in the surface or near-surface regions.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit38"><sup><span class="sup_ref">38</span></sup></a> This results in superior rate capabilities for MS5 compared to MS1, as previously demonstrated.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">The Nyquist plots and DRT analysis results for MS1–MS5 are presented in Fig. S19.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> The Nyquist plots of all five sulfides show low- and mid-frequency arcs, as well as high-frequency straight lines. The low-frequency straight line is attributed to Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> diffusion processes, the mid-frequency arc represents charge-transfer processes at the electrode–electrolyte interface, and the high-frequency arcs are associated with the formation of the SEI.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit29"><sup><span class="sup_ref">29</span></sup></a> The DRT relaxation time (<span class="italic">τ</span>) distribution provides kinetic information about electrochemical reactions. Based on the value of <span class="italic">τ</span>, different electrochemical processes can be identified. The areas under the peaks reflect the resistance in electrochemical reactions, where peaks with <span class="italic">τ</span> &gt; 1 s correspond to the diffusion impedance <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>d</sub></small>, peaks with 10<small><sup>−3</sup></small> s &lt; <span class="italic">τ</span> &lt; 1 s correspond to the charge-transfer impedance <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>ct</sub></small>, and peaks with <span class="italic">τ</span> &lt; 10<small><sup>−3</sup></small> s represent the interfacial impedance <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>s</sub></small>.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit39"><sup><span class="sup_ref">39,40</span></sup></a> The <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>d</sub></small>, <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>ct</sub></small> and <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>s</sub></small> values obtained for each sulfide are shown in Table S3.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> The <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>d</sub></small>, <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>ct</sub></small>, and <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>s</sub></small> values for MS1 are 75.28, 2.223, and 2.2 Ω, respectively, while for MS5, they are 57.454, 0.539, and 0.597 Ω (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4e</a>). The lower impedance of MS5 facilitates the synergistic transport of electrons/ions, accelerating the kinetic processes, thereby resulting in superior electrochemical performance.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">Subsequently, the sodium-ion diffusion coefficients during the initial cycling processes of MS1 and MS5 were analysed using the GITT (Fig. S20<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>). The Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> diffusion coefficients (<span class="italic">D</span><small><sub>Na<small><sup>+</sup></small></sub></small>) were estimated using a simplified version of Fick's Second Law. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4f</a> shows that <span class="italic">D</span><small><sub>Na<small><sup>+</sup></small></sub></small> during MS1 discharge varies between 10<small><sup>−10.3</sup></small> and 10<small><sup>−14</sup></small> cm<small><sup>2</sup></small> s<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, while for MS5, these values range between 10<small><sup>−9.6</sup></small> and 10<small><sup>−12.7</sup></small> cm<small><sup>2</sup></small> s<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. During charging, MS1 and MS5 show <span class="italic">D</span><small><sub>Na<small><sup>+</sup></small></sub></small> values of 10<small><sup>−10.7</sup></small>–10<small><sup>−12.6</sup></small> and 10<small><sup>−10</sup></small>–10<small><sup>−11.4</sup></small> cm<small><sup>2</sup></small> s<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, respectively. Overall, MS5 exhibits <span class="italic">D</span><small><sub>Na<small><sup>+</sup></small></sub></small> values approximately one order of magnitude higher than those of MS1, indicating that the high-entropy-induced disordered structure promotes sodium-ion diffusion within the material.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">In order to simulate a battery system that more closely resembles real-world application conditions and to ensure that the overall performance, stability, and safety of the battery meet practical requirements, we chose carbonate-based electrolytes with a wider electrochemical window (0–3.6 V) to assemble the full cells. Using NVP as the cathode and MS5 as the anode, a whole battery was assembled in a 1 M NaClO<small><sub>4</sub></small> (PC + 5% FEC) electrolyte to further evaluate the practicality of HES materials as SIB anodes. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4g</a> shows the rate capabilities of the whole battery at different C rates. Specifically, after 10 cycles at C rates of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 5, and 10 (1C = 117 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>), the whole battery exhibits specific capacities of 92.4, 98.5, 102.7, 103.4, 102.3, 90.4, and 76.2 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, respectively. The increase in the specific capacity at lower C rates is attributed to the anode storing excess sodium after the activation of the half-cell. Encouragingly, after high-rate charge–discharge cycles, the whole battery still maintained a specific capacity of 95.6 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> over 10 cycles at 0.1C, indicating excellent rate capability.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">Subsequently, the cycling stability of the whole battery was tested at 1C. As shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4h</a>, the initial discharge specific capacity of the whole battery is 96.6 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, which reduces to only 96.2 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> after 100 cycles (capacity retention rate close to 100%), thereby demonstrating excellent cycling stability. However, the CE of the whole battery is low during the first 10 cycles, possibly because of the consumption of sodium ions during the formation of the cathode electrolyte interphase. In subsequent cycles, the CE of the whole battery stabilised at approximately 98%. In summary, the HES material shows promising prospects for practical energy-storage applications.</p>
      
      
        
        <h3 id="sect698"><span class="b_heading">3.3 Highly reversible sodium storage in the high-entropy anode material</span></h3>
        <span>To further comprehend the structural evolution of the HES, SEM images of slices of the MS1–MS5 electrodes before cycling show interconnected spherical particles bridged by the sodium alginate binder (Fig. S21<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>). After 100 cycles (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5a–d</a> and S22<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>), MS1–MS4 exhibit some degree of pulverisation, whereas MS5 remains in its initial state. At the 25 μm scale, significant and widespread cracking can be observed in the MS1 electrode, which may lead to the detachment of the active material from the current collector, resulting in MS1 exhibiting the poorest electrochemical performance. At the 1 μm scale, MS5 displays line-like structures and nanoparticles ranging from tens to hundreds of nanometers in size, dispersed randomly across the electrode surface. This nanoscale structure and distribution provide greater buffering space to accommodate volume expansion. This indicates that the introduction of high-entropy structures can mitigate volume expansion during cycling, thereby extending the cycle life of batteries. EDS maps of MS5 after 100 cycles (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5e</a>) show a uniform distribution of Fe, Co, Ni, Cu, Zn, In, and S on the electrode surface, with no signs of aggregation. In contrast, in MS1, cracks in the material are rich in Fe and In, but poor in S. These findings illustrate the high reaction reversibility conferred by the high-entropy effect in sulfides.</span>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig5"><a href="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta05122j/d4ta05122j-f5_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta05122j-f5.tif" src="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta05122j/d4ta05122j-f5.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 5 </b> <span id="fig5"><span class="graphic_title">SEM images of (a) and (c) MS1 and (b) and (d) MS5 after 100 cycles. (e) EDS diagrams of MS5 after 100 cycles.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <p class="otherpara">The surface elemental oxidation states and contents in MS5 before and after cycling were compared. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6</a> shows the XPS images of MS5 before and after cycling. Characteristic peaks of all elements are observed in the full spectra. In the Fe 2p XPS spectra, peaks at 703.45 and 723.2 eV correspond to Fe<small><sup>2+</sup></small> 2p<small><sub>3/2</sub></small> and 2p<small><sub>1/2</sub></small>, respectively.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit41"><sup><span class="sup_ref">41,42</span></sup></a> Peaks at 713.2 and 726.8 eV correspond to Fe<small><sup>3+</sup></small> 2p<small><sub>3/2</sub></small> and 2p<small><sub>1/2</sub></small>, respectively, while peaks at 717.4 and 733.1 eV are the satellite peaks of Fe.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit43"><sup><span class="sup_ref">43</span></sup></a> Fe on the surface of MS5 exists in both 3+ and 2+ oxidation states, with an Fe<small><sup>3+</sup></small> content of ∼11.5%. In the Co 2p XPS spectra, peaks at 781.2 and 796.6 eV are attributed to Co<small><sup>2+</sup></small> 2p<small><sub>3/2</sub></small> and 2p<small><sub>1/2</sub></small>, respectively, peaks at 777.4 and 792.8 eV correspond to Co<small><sup>0</sup></small> 2p<small><sub>3/2</sub></small> and 2p<small><sub>1/2</sub></small>, respectively, and peaks at 778.3 and 793.7 eV correspond to Co<small><sup>3+</sup></small> 2p<small><sub>3/2</sub></small> and 2p<small><sub>1/2</sub></small>, respectively. Additionally, peaks at 783.2, 786.7, 798.5, and 802.3 eV are the satellite peaks of Co.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit44"><sup><span class="sup_ref">44</span></sup></a> In the Ni spectra, peaks at 856.6 and 874.4 eV correspond to Ni<small><sup>3+</sup></small> 2p<small><sub>3/2</sub></small> and 2p<small><sub>1/2</sub></small>, respectively, while peaks at 852.9 and 870.7 eV correspond to Ni<small><sup>0</sup></small> 2p<small><sub>3/2</sub></small> and 2p<small><sub>1/2</sub></small>, respectively. Peaks at 861.4, 863.9, 879.7, and 883.4 eV are the satellite peaks of Ni.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit45"><sup><span class="sup_ref">45,46</span></sup></a> In the Cu spectra, peaks at 931.5 and 951.4 eV correspond to Cu<small><sup>+</sup></small> 2p<small><sub>3/2</sub></small> and 2p<small><sub>1/2</sub></small>, respectively, while peaks at 935 and 954.9 eV correspond to Cu<small><sup>2+</sup></small> 2p<small><sub>3/2</sub></small> and 2p<small><sub>1/2</sub></small>, respectively.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit47"><sup><span class="sup_ref">47,48</span></sup></a> Additionally, peaks at 940.5, 942.8, 946.4, and 961.5 eV are the satellite peaks of Cu. In the Zn spectra, peaks at 1021.9 and 1045 eV correspond to Zn<small><sub>2+</sub></small> 2p<small><sub>3/2</sub></small> and 2p<small><sub>1/2</sub></small>, respectively.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit49"><sup><span class="sup_ref">49</span></sup></a> In the S 2p spectra, peaks at 161.3 and 162.8 eV correspond to metal–sulfur bonds (M–S, M = Fe, Co, <span class="italic">etc.</span>) and M–S<small><sub>2</sub></small> (S<small><sub>2</sub></small> means disulfide), respectively. The positive shift in S 2p suggests a reduction in electron density around sulfur, confirming the successful formation of sulfur vacancies (<span class="italic">vs.</span> MS1, Fig. S23<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>). These vacancies indicate a decrease in anion numbers, resulting in an increased electron cloud density around cations, which is reflected by the higher binding energy of sulfur. Moreover, the energy of S 2p<small><sub>1/2</sub></small> is attributed to low-coordinated sulfur vacancies.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit50"><sup><span class="sup_ref">50–53</span></sup></a> The ratio of the S 2p<small><sub>1/2</sub></small> peak area of MS5 increased by 3.0% (from 33.4% to 36.4%) before and after the reaction. This increase can be attributed to the generation of sulfur vacancies due to structural changes in HES during the extraction and insertion of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>, which also confirms the cycling performance of the HES. In MS5, the ionic concentrations of Co<small><sup>2+</sup></small>, Co<small><sup>3+</sup></small>, Ni<small><sup>3+</sup></small>, and Cu<small><sup>+</sup></small> are 60.3%, 17.4%, 82.9%, and 88.7%, respectively. The abundance of trivalent ions facilitates electron transition and transmission during the charge and discharge processes.</p>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig6"><a href="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta05122j/d4ta05122j-f6_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta05122j-f6.tif" src="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta05122j/d4ta05122j-f6.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 6 </b> <span id="fig6"><span class="graphic_title">XPS spectra for Fe 2p, Co 2p, Ni 2p, Cu 2p, Zn 2p and S 2p of the MS5 anode before and after 100 cycles.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <p class="otherpara">The XPS spectra of MS5 after 100 cycles reveal that the characteristic peaks of Fe, Co, Ni, Cu, Zn and S are similar to those before cycling (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6</a>), indicating the high reversibility and structural stability of the HES during cycling. After cycling, Fe was present in the form of Fe<small><sup>2+</sup></small> and Fe<small><sup>3+</sup></small>, with Fe<small><sup>3+</sup></small> accounting for 36.57% of Fe ions. Co was present as elemental Co, Co<small><sup>2+</sup></small>, and Co<small><sup>3+</sup></small>, with Co<small><sup>2+</sup></small> and Co<small><sup>3+</sup></small> accounting for 31.36% and 19.12% of the total ions. Ni was found in the form of Ni<small><sup>2+</sup></small> and Ni<small><sup>3+</sup></small>, with Ni<small><sup>3+</sup></small> constituting 49.86% of the total, whereas Cu was predominantly in the form of Cu<small><sup>+</sup></small>. Compared with the initial MS5 material, higher Fe<small><sup>3+</sup></small>, Co<small><sup>0</sup></small>, and Ni<small><sup>2+</sup></small> contents were observed after cycling, and the disappearance of Ni<small><sup>0</sup></small>. A higher trivalent ion content facilitates electron transition and transmission, thereby maintaining superior electrochemical performance. The changes in the Co<small><sup>0</sup></small> and Ni<small><sup>0</sup></small> contents may be attributable to the higher reactivity of Ni in MS5 than that of Co. During charging, Ni is preferentially oxidised over Co, leading to the accumulation of elemental Co and the coarsening of Co nanoparticles, which could affect the electrochemical performance of the material. As previously discussed, Cu diffuses into the active substance, participates in electrochemical reactions, and contributes to the capacity. As shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6</a>, no Cu<small><sup>2+</sup></small> or Cu<small><sup>0</sup></small> is detected after cycling. However, the use of a Cu current collector means that the electrode is consistently in a copper-rich state. Thus, it is inferred that apart from Cu in the current collector participating in the transformation reaction of the HES, some Cu also reacts independently with Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>S.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit28"><sup><span class="sup_ref">28,54</span></sup></a> The appearance of a new voltage plateau near 1.5 V in the charge–discharge curves of the five sulfides after 100 cycles (Fig. S5<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> and <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">3b</a>) further supports this conclusion. Table S4<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> summarises the major changes in the elemental oxidation states of MS5 before and after cycling.</p>
      
      
        
        <h3 id="sect789"><span class="b_heading">3.4 DFT simulations of the sulfide materials</span></h3>
        <span>First, configuration convergence tests of the MS1–MS5 sulfide materials were conducted, and the converged cutoff energies and <span class="italic">k</span>-points were chosen for geometry optimisation. The optimised configurations from the geometry optimisation were used as models for subsequent calculations.</span>
        <p class="otherpara">
          <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig7">Fig. 7a</a> presents the band structure of MS5 with the Fermi level set as the energy reference point. The valence band maximum was 0.1905 eV above the Fermi level, and the conduction band minimum was 0.0966 eV below the Fermi level. MS5 exhibits metallic characteristics. MS1–MS4 are indirect bandgap semiconductors, with the valence band maximum located at the L point and the conduction band minimum at the G point. During an indirect transition, electrons require the involvement of quantized lattice vibrational energy (phonons) to be excited from the valence band maximum to the conduction band minimum, which hinders the generation of electrons and holes (Fig. S24<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>). The electrical conductivity of semiconductor materials depends on their bandgap size. A smaller bandgap means that electrons can be excited at lower temperatures or with less energy input, leading to higher electrical conductivity. With an increase in the number of element types used to dope the base material, the bandgap energies of the sulfide materials decrease significantly. The introduction of high-entropy structures reduces the bandgap of the sulfide materials, facilitating rapid electron transfer during the charging and discharging processes, thus ensuring excellent electrochemical performance.</p>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig7"><a href="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta05122j/d4ta05122j-f7_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4ta05122j-f7.tif" src="/image/article/2024/TA/d4ta05122j/d4ta05122j-f7.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 7 </b> <span id="fig7"><span class="graphic_title">(a) Energy band diagrams of MS5. (b) Density of states and fractional density of states diagrams of MS5. (c) Electron differential charge density plots of MS5. (d) Schematic of the volume change for MS1 to Na<small><sub>2.5</sub></small>MS1. (e) Schematic of the volume change in the conversion of MS5 to Na<small><sub>2.5</sub></small>MS5. (f) Possible adsorption sites for a Na atom in the MS5 material.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <p class="otherpara">The total density of states and partial density of states (PDOS) of MS5 and MS1–MS4 are shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig7">Fig. 7b</a> and S25,<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> respectively. The PDOS graphs of each material show resonance peaks for S s-orbitals and In p-orbitals at around −15.4 to −14.0 eV, respectively. Resonance peaks are also present for S s-orbitals and p-orbitals of the transition metal elements introduced at tetrahedral sites at around −13.4 to −11.0 eV. Significant resonance peaks are observed near the Fermi level for the p-orbitals and d-orbitals of the transition metal elements at the tetrahedral sites, as well as for the S p orbitals, indicating strong bonding effects between In and S and between tetrahedral sites and S, thus maintaining the stability of the crystal structure composed of tetrahedra and octahedra. Among the five sulfides, the differences in the DOS for In and S are minimal. In MS5, the energy band near the Fermi level mainly originates from the d-orbitals at the tetrahedral sites, suggesting that the presence of Fe and Co has little influence on the DOS near the Fermi level. In contrast, Ni, Cu, and Zn in the sulfides can form stronger bonds and provide stronger electron localisation. The DOS of MS5 is significantly higher than those of the other four sulfides, possibly because of the random distribution of multiple metal elements at the tetrahedral sites, which reduces the symmetry of the material and increases the local disorder in the system. The introduction of high-entropy structures can markedly increase the electron density and enhance the rate performance of sulfides. Moreover, the d-orbitals of five elements appear at similar positions near the Fermi level, indicating overlapping d-orbitals,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit55"><sup><span class="sup_ref">55</span></sup></a> which can enhance electron transfer and thereby improve the electrochemical performance of MS2–MS5 compared to MS1.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">
          <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig7">Fig. 7c</a> and S26<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> show the differential charge density maps of MS5 and MS1–MS4, respectively. The maps use different colours to indicate regions of distinct charge densities, where blue regions represent charge depletion, red regions represent charge accumulation, and white regions indicate no change in charge. In MS1, Fe atoms at the tetrahedral sites donate electrons, and S atoms gain electrons, with electrons accumulating around the S atoms to form a tetrahedral structure. There is almost no electron distribution around the In atoms, suggesting that the In atoms maintain a stable spinel framework during the charging and discharging processes in MS1. With the introduction of elements such as Co and Ni, the number of electrons provided by the tetrahedral sites decreases, resulting in stronger electron accumulation. These atoms exhibit redox activity and participate in the gain and loss of electrons. This may be due to the increased configurational entropy of the system stabilising the crystal structure, allowing some In atoms to maintain the framework stability and become reactive, thereby creating more active sites. In particular, when the system reaches a high-entropy configuration, the electron cloud density of the high-entropy material is higher than those of the other four sulfides, resulting in a stronger electron-donating ability, increasing the number of intercalated/deintercalated Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>, and enhancing the capacity of the battery.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">According to the literature, metal sulfides are conversion-type electrode materials, and their sodium-storage mechanism generally involves the insertion of sodium ions, followed by a conversion reaction, ultimately forming Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>S and doped transition metals.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit56"><sup><span class="sup_ref">56–59</span></sup></a> DFT calculations were performed to determine the volumetric changes and corresponding reaction potentials during the intercalation reaction of the sulfide materials. MS5 with lattice parameters <span class="italic">a</span> = <span class="italic">b</span> = <span class="italic">c</span> = 7.453 Å and a unit cell volume of 414 Å<small><sup>3</sup></small> was used as the base model. This crystal contains three stable sodium-atom adsorption sites: T, O, and TO. The mechanism diagram in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig7">Fig. 7f</a> shows that MS5 exhibits excellent sodium-storage performance at the atomic scale. To study the relationship between binding energies and the Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> insertion sites in MS5 at different discharge stages, energy optimisations were conducted for various sodium intercalation structures. The formation energies of the intermediate phases during the discharge process, calculated using <a title="" href="#eqn3">eqn (3)</a>, are −2.256, −2.430, −2.368, −1.972, and −2.388 eV for Na<small><sub>0.5</sub></small>MS5, NaMS5, Na<small><sub>1.5</sub></small>MS5, Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>MS5, and Na<small><sub>2.5</sub></small>MS5, respectively. Δ<span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>f</sub></small> being negative is favorable for the intercalation reaction of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>. With further discharge, the sodium-ion insertion sites may become closer to the S atoms, causing the tetrahedral X–S (X = Fe, Co, Ni, Cu, Zn) bonds to break and form Na–S bonds, eventually leading to the conversion of the bulk material to Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>S and doped transition metals. Table S5<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> lists the relationship between the formation energies and reaction potentials of MS5 after Na adsorption. Accordingly, in practical applications, using a test voltage range of 0–2.5 V can meet the charging and discharging voltage requirements of MS5. Additionally, the theoretical voltage plateau at around 0.5 V closely matches the initial discharge curve voltage plateau of MS5 at 0.2 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> (Fig. S27<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>), validating the rationality of theoretical calculations.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">The insertion of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> causes severe volumetric deformation in sulfide anodes, leading to the dislodgment of the active substance from the current collector and resulting in significant capacity and lifespan degradation. We further simulated the rate of volumetric change of the insertion reaction for MS5 (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig7">Fig. 7e</a>). In Na<small><sub>1.5</sub></small>MS5, some X–S and In–S bonds break, while two broken X–S bonds form X–X bonds. With further sodium insertion, the X–S bonds recover, whereas all In–S bonds break. During these two reaction steps, the volumetric deformation exceeds 10%. In Na<small><sub>2.5</sub></small>MS5, no X–S bonds break and no new ones are formed, so the corresponding volumetric change is relatively small. This indicates that the breaking or forming of chemical bonds during Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> intercalation/deintercalation is the primary factor causing the significant volumetric expansion of the sulfide materials studied here during cycling. Overall, the volumetric change of the HES in this process was 58.0%, which is significantly lower than that of MS1 (65.2%; <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig7">Fig. 7d</a>). To verify the simulation results mentioned above, we conducted SEM tests on the cross-sections of MS1 and MS5 electrodes before and after cycling (Fig. S28<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>). The results showed that MS1 exhibited more significant volume expansion compared to MS5. This demonstrates that the high-entropy structure can mitigate volumetric expansion and maintain structural stability, thus enhancing the electrochemical performance of sulfides.</p>
      
    
    
      
      <h2 id="sect838"><span class="a_heading">4 Conclusion</span></h2>
      <span>In this study, MS5 HES was successfully synthesized using a simple co-precipitation method followed by high-temperature sintering and applied as an anode material in SIBs. Benefiting from the introduction of four transition metal elements, MS5 HES exhibited sulfur vacancies and reduced crystal size, which in turn promoted an increase in active sites and enhanced the conductivity of the electrode material. Meanwhile, DFT calculation results also confirmed that the introduction of a high-entropy structure significantly reduced the band gap of the sulfide material, increased the electron cloud density around the tetrahedra, and enhanced the reactivity of the In element at octahedral sites, facilitating Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> transport within the anode material. This resulted in superior rate performance and cycling stability. Compared to medium and low-entropy sulfides, HES exhibits a lower reaction voltage platform and smaller volume changes, which are beneficial for increasing the working voltage and energy density of the whole battery, as well as extending the battery lifespan. This study provides valuable insights into the Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> storage mechanism and offers a “high-entropy” approach for addressing the issue of volume expansion in sulfide anodes during cycling.</span>

            <br><a href="https://cookiepedia.co.uk/giving-consent-to-cookies" class="privacy-notice-link" rel="noopener" target="_blank" aria-label="More information about your privacy, opens in a new tab">More information</a></div><button id="accept-recommended-btn-handler">Allow all</button><section class="ot-sdk-row ot-cat-grp"><h3 id="ot-category-title"> Manage consent preferences</h3><div class="ot-pli-hdr ot-leg-border-color"><span class="ot-li-title">Consent</span> <span class="ot-li-title">Legitimate interest</span></div><div class="ot-accordion-layout ot-cat-item ot-vs-config" data-optanongroupid="C0001"><button aria-expanded="false" ot-accordion="true" aria-controls="ot-desc-id-C0001" aria-labelledby="ot-header-id-C0001 ot-status-id-C0001"></button><!-- Accordion header --><div class="ot-acc-hdr"><div class="ot-plus-minus"><span></span><span></span></div><h4 class="ot-cat-header" id="ot-header-id-C0001">Strictly necessary cookies</h4></div><!-- accordion detail --><div class="ot-acc-grpcntr ot-acc-txt"><p class="ot-acc-grpdesc ot-category-desc" id="ot-desc-id-C0001">These cookies are necessary for the website to function and cannot be switched off in our systems. They are usually only set in response to actions made by you which amount to a request for services, such as setting your privacy preferences, logging in or filling in forms. You can set your browser to block or alert you about these cookies, but some parts of the site will not then work. These cookies do not store any personally identifiable information.</p></div></div><div class="ot-accordion-layout ot-cat-item ot-vs-config" data-optanongroupid="C0002"><button aria-expanded="false" ot-accordion="true" aria-controls="ot-desc-id-C0002" aria-labelledby="ot-header-id-C0002"></button><!-- Accordion header --><div class="ot-acc-hdr"><div class="ot-plus-minus"><span></span><span></span></div><h4 class="ot-cat-header" id="ot-header-id-C0002">Analytical cookies</h4><div class="ot-tgl"><input type="checkbox" name="ot-group-id-C0002" id="ot-group-id-C0002" role="switch" class="category-switch-handler" data-optanongroupid="C0002" aria-labelledby="ot-header-id-C0002"> <label class="ot-switch" for="ot-group-id-C0002"><span class="ot-switch-nob"></span> <span class="ot-label-txt">Analytical cookies</span></label> </div></div><!-- accordion detail --><div class="ot-acc-grpcntr ot-acc-txt"><p class="ot-acc-grpdesc ot-category-desc" id="ot-desc-id-C0002">These cookies allow us to count visits and traffic sources so we can measure and improve the performance of our site. They help us to know which pages are the most and least popular and see how visitors move around the site. All information these cookies collect is aggregated and therefore anonymous. If you do not allow these cookies we will not know when you have visited our site, and will not be able to monitor its performance.</p></div></div><div class="ot-accordion-layout ot-cat-item ot-vs-config" data-optanongroupid="C0003"><button aria-expanded="false" ot-accordion="true" aria-controls="ot-desc-id-C0003" aria-labelledby="ot-header-id-C0003"></button><!-- Accordion header --><div class="ot-acc-hdr"><div class="ot-plus-minus"><span></span><span></span></div><h4 class="ot-cat-header" id="ot-header-id-C0003">Functional cookies</h4><div class="ot-tgl"><input type="checkbox" name="ot-group-id-C0003" id="ot-group-id-C0003" role="switch" class="category-switch-handler" data-optanongroupid="C0003" aria-labelledby="ot-header-id-C0003"> <label class="ot-switch" for="ot-group-id-C0003"><span class="ot-switch-nob"></span> <span class="ot-label-txt">Functional cookies</span></label> </div></div><!-- accordion detail --><div class="ot-acc-grpcntr ot-acc-txt"><p class="ot-acc-grpdesc ot-category-desc" id="ot-desc-id-C0003">These cookies enable the website to provide enhanced functionality and personalisation.
